8 Concurrency
Concurrency
Our focus so far has been on semantics for sequential computation. But
the world is not sequential...
â€¢ hardware is intrinsically parallel (fine-grain, across words, to
coarse-grain, e.g. multiple execution units)
â€¢ multi-processor machines
â€¢ multi-threading (perhaps on a single processor)
â€¢ networked machines
113
Problems
â€¢ the state-spaces of our systems become large, with the combinatorial
explosion â€“ with n threads, each of which can be in 2 states, the
system has 2n states.
â€¢ the state-spaces become complex
â€¢ computation becomes nondeterministic (unless synchrony is
imposed), as different threads/machines/... operate at different
speeds.
â€¢ parallel components competing for access to resources may deadlock
or suffer starvation. Need mutual exclusion between components
accessing a resource.
More Problems!
â€¢ partial failure (of some processes, of some machines in a network, of
some persistent storage devices). Need transactional mechanisms.
â€¢ communication between different environments (with different local
resources (e.g. different local stores, or libraries, or...)
â€¢ partial version change
â€¢ communication between administrative regions with partial trust (or,
indeed, no trust); protection against mailicious attack.
â€¢ dealing with contingent complexity (embedded historical accidents;
upwards-compatible deltas)
Theme: as for sequential languages, but much more so, itâ€™s a complicated
world.
Aim of this lecture: just to give you a taste of how a little semantics can
be used to express some of the fine distinctions. Primarily (1) to boost
your intuition for informal reasoning, but also (2) this can support rigorous
proof about really hairy crypto protocols, cache-coherency protocols,
comms, database transactions,....
Going to define the simplest possible (well, almost) concurrent language,
call it L1, and explore a few issues. Youâ€™ve seen most of them informally
in CSAA.
114
Booleans b âˆˆ B = {true, false}
Integers n âˆˆ Z = {...,âˆ’1, 0, 1, ...}
Locations â„“ âˆˆ L = {l , l0, l1, l2, ...}
Operations op ::= + |â‰¥
Expressions
e ::= n | b | e1 op e2 | if e1 then e2 else e3 |
â„“ := e |!â„“ |
skip | e1; e2 |
while e1 do e2|
e1 e2
T ::= int | bool | unit | proc
Tloc ::= intref
Parallel Composition: Typing and Reduction
(thread) Î“ âŠ¢ e:unit
Î“ âŠ¢ e:proc
(parallel) Î“ âŠ¢ e1:proc Î“ âŠ¢ e2:proc

Morals
â€¢ There really is a combinatorial explosion â€“ and you donâ€™t want to be

â€¢ Drawing state-space diagrams only works for really tiny examples â€“ we

â€¢ Almost certainly you (as the programmer) didnâ€™t want all those 3
outcomes to be possible â€“ need better idioms or constructs for

cf. Lamportâ€™s â€œBakeryâ€ algorithm from Concurrent Systems and

Locking Disciplines
So, suppose we have several programs e1, ..., ek, all well-typed with
Î“ âŠ¢ ei:unit, that we want to execute concurrently without â€˜interferenceâ€™
(whatever that is). Think of them as transaction bodies.
There are many possible locking disciplines. Weâ€™ll focus on one, to see
how it â€“ and the properties it guarantees â€“ can be made precise and
proved.
An Ordered 2PL Discipline, Informally
Fix an association between locations and mutexes. For simplicity, make it
1:1 â€“ associate l with m, l1 with m1, etc.
Fix a lock acquisition order. For simplicity, make it m, m0, m1, m2, ....
Require that each ei
â€¢ acquires the lock mj for each location lj it uses, before it uses it
â€¢ acquires and releases each lock in a properly-bracketed way
â€¢ does not acquire any lock after itâ€™s released any lock (two-phase)
â€¢ acquires locks in increasing order
Then, informally, (e1 ... ek) should (a) never deadlock, and (b) be
serialisable â€“ any execution of it should be â€˜equivalentâ€™ to an execution of
eÏ€(1); ...; eÏ€(k) for some permutation Ï€.
These are semantic properties again. In general, it wonâ€™t be computable whether they hold.
For simple ei, though, itâ€™s often obvious. Further, one can construct syntactic disciplines
that are checkable and are sufficient to guarantee these.
See Transactional Information Systems, Gerhard Weikum and Gottfried Vossen, for much
more detail on locking disciplines etc. (albeit not from a programming-language semantics
perspective).

The Theorem

points at which each ei acquires its final lock. That defines a serialisation
order. In between times, consider commutativity of actions of the different
ei â€“ the premises guarantee that many actions are semantically
independent, and so can be permuted.
Weâ€™ve not discussed fairness â€“ the semantics allows any interleaving
between parallel components, not only fair ones.
Language Properties
(Obviously!) donâ€™t have Determinacy.
Still have Type Preservation.
Have Progress, but it has to be modified â€“ a well-typed expression of type
proc will reduce to some parallel composition of unit values.
Typing and type inference is scarcely changed.
(very fancy type systems can be used to enforce locking disciplines)

What can you use semantics for?

programmer; what you must provide as a compiler writer
2. as a tool for language design:

and how they interact.

A How To Do Proofs
The purpose of this handout is give a general guide as to how to prove theorems. This
should give you some help in answering questions that begin with â€œShow that the following
is true . . . â€. It is based on notes by Myra VanInwegen, with additional text added by Peter
Sewell in Â§A.1. Many thanks to Myra for making her original notes available.
The focus here is on doing informal but rigorous proofs. These are rather different from
the formal proofs, in Natural Deduction or Sequent Calculus, that were introduced in the
Logic and Proof course. Formal proofs are derivations in one of those proof systems â€“ they
are in a completely well-defined form, but are often far too verbose to deal with by hand
(although they can be machine-checked). Informal proofs, on the other hand, are the usual
mathematical notion of proof: written arguments to persuade the reader that you could, if
pushed, write a fully formal proof.
This is important for two reasons. Most obviously, you should learn how to do these proofs.
More subtly, but more importantly, only by working with the mathematical definitions in
some way can you develop a good intuition for what they mean â€” trying to do some proofs
is the best way of understanding the definitions.
A.1 How to go about it
Proofs differ, but for many of those you meet the following steps should be helpful.
1. Make sure the statement of the conjecture is precisely defined. In particular, make
sure you understand any strange notation, and find the definitions of all the auxiliary
gadgets involved (e.g. definitions of any typing or reduction relations mentioned in the
statement, or any other predicates or functions).
2. Try to understand at an intuitive level what the conjecture is saying â€“ verbalize out
loud the basic point. For example, for a Type Preservation conjecture, the basic
point might be something like â€œif a well-typed configuration reduces, the result is still
well-typed (with the same type)â€.
3. Try to understand intuitively why it is true (or false...). Identify what the most
interesting cases might be â€” the cases that you think are most likely to be suspicious,
or hard to prove. Sometimes itâ€™s good to start with the easy cases (if the setting
is unfamiliar to you); sometimes itâ€™s good to start with the hard cases (to find any
interesting problems as soon as possible).
4. Think of a good basic strategy. This might be:
(a) simple logic manipulations;
(b) collecting together earlier results, again by simple logic; or
(c) some kind of induction.
5. Try it! (remembering you might have to backtrack if you discover you picked a strategy
that doesnâ€™t work well for this conjecture). This might involve any of the following:
(a) Expanding definitions, inlining them. Sometimes you can just blindly expand all
definitions, but more often itâ€™s important to expand only the definitions which
you want to work with the internal structure of â€” otherwise things just get too
verbose.
(b) Making abbreviations â€” defining a new variable to stand for some complex gadget
youâ€™re working with, saying e.g.
where e = (let x:int = 7+2 in x+x)
Take care with choosing variable names.
126
(c) Doing equational reasoning, e.g.
e = e1 by ...
= e2 by ...
= e3 as ...
Here the e might be any mathematical object â€” arithmetic expressions, or ex-
pressions of some grammar, or formulae. Some handy equations over formulae
are given in Â§A.2.2.
(d) Proving a formula based on its structure. For example, to prove a formula âˆ€x âˆˆ
S.P (x) you would often assume you have an arbitrary x and then try to prove
P (x).
Take an arbitrary x âˆˆ S.
We now have to show P (x):
This is covered in detail in Â§A.2.3. Much proof is of this form, automatically
driven by the structure of the formula.
(e) Using an assumption youâ€™ve made above.
(f) Induction. As covered in the 1B Semantics notes, there are various kinds of induc-
tion you might want to use: mathematical induction over the natural numbers,
structural induction over the elements of some grammar, or rule induction over
the rules defining some relation (especially a reduction or typing relation). For
each, you should:
i. Decide (and state!) what kind of induction youâ€™re using. This may need
some thought and experience, and you might have to backtrack.
ii. Remind yourself what the induction principle is exactly.
iii. Decide on the induction hypothesis youâ€™re going to use, writing down a pred-
icate Î¦ which is such that the conclusion of the induction principle implies
the thing youâ€™re trying to prove. Again, this might need some thought. Take
care with the quantifiers here â€” itâ€™s suspicious if your definition of Î¦ has
any globally-free variables...
iv. Go through each of the premises of the induction principle and prove each one
(using any of these techniques as appropriate). Many of those premises will
be implications, e.g. âˆ€x âˆˆ N.Î¦(x)â‡’ Î¦(x + 1), for which you can do a proof
based on the structure of the formula â€” taking an arbitrary x, assuming
Î¦(x), and trying to prove Î¦(x+1). Usually at some point in the latter youâ€™d
make use of the assumption Î¦(x).
6. In all of the above, remember: the point of doing a proof on paper is to use the
formalism to help you think â€” to help you cover all cases, precisely â€” and also to
communicate with the reader. For both, you need to write clearly:
(a) Use enough words! â€œAssumeâ€, â€œWe have to showâ€, â€œBy such-and-such we knowâ€,
â€œHenceâ€,...
(b) Donâ€™t use random squiggles. Itâ€™s good to have formulae properly nested within
text, with and no â€œâ‡’â€ or â€œâˆ´â€ between lines of text.
7. If it hasnâ€™t worked yet... either
(a) youâ€™ve make some local mistake, e.g. mis-instantiated something, or used the
same variable for two different things, or not noticed that you have a definition
you should have expanded or an assumption you should have used. Fix it and
continue.
127
(b) youâ€™ve discovered that the conjecture is really false. Usually at this point itâ€™s
a good idea to construct a counterexample that is as simple as possible, and to
check carefully that it really is a counterexample.
(c) you need to try a different strategy â€” often, to use a different induction principle
or to strengthen your induction hypothesis.
(d) you didnâ€™t really understand intuitively what the conjecture is saying, or what
the definitions it uses mean. Go back to them again.
8. If it has worked: read through it, skeptically, and check. Maybe youâ€™ll need to re-write
it to make it comprehensible: proof discovery is not the same as proof exposition. See
the example proofs in the Semantics notes.
9. Finally, give it to someone else, as skeptical and careful as you can find, to see if they
believe it â€” to see if they believe that what youâ€™ve written down is a proof, not that
they believe that the conjecture is true.
128
A.2 And in More Detail...
First, Iâ€™ll explain informal proof intuitively, giving a couple of examples. Then Iâ€™ll explain
how this intuition is reflected in the sequent rules from Logic and Proof.
In the following, Iâ€™ll call any logic statement a formula. In general, what weâ€™ll be trying to
do is prove a formula, using a collection of formulas that we know to be true or are assuming
to be true. Thereâ€™s a big difference between using a formula and proving a formula. In fact,
what you do is in many ways opposite. So, Iâ€™ll start by explaining how to prove a formula.
A.2.1 Meet the Connectives
Here are the logical connectives and a very brief decription of what each means.
P âˆ§Q P and Q are both true
P âˆ¨Q P is true, or Q is true, or both are true
Â¬P P is not true (P is false)
P â‡’ Q if P is true then Q is true
P â‡” Q P is true exactly when Q is true
âˆ€x âˆˆ S.P (x) for all x in S, P is true of x
âˆƒx âˆˆ S.P (x) there exists an x in S such that P holds of x
A.2.2 Equivalences
These are formulas that mean the same thing, and this is indicated by a â‰ƒ between them.
The fact that they are equivalent to each other is justified by the truth tables of the con-
nectives.
definition of â‡’ P â‡’ Q â‰ƒ Â¬P âˆ¨Q
definition of â‡” P â‡” Q â‰ƒ (P â‡’ Q) âˆ§ (Qâ‡’ P )

distributive laws P âˆ¨ (Q âˆ§R) â‰ƒ (P âˆ¨Q) âˆ§ (P âˆ¨R)

(âˆƒx.P (x)) âˆ¨Q â‰ƒ (âˆƒx.P (x) âˆ¨Q)
A.2.3 How to Prove a Formula
For each of the logical connectives, Iâ€™ll explain how to handle them.
âˆ€x âˆˆ S.P (x) This means â€œFor all x in S, P is true of x.â€ Such a formula is called a
universally quantified formula. The goal is to prove that the property P , which has some
xs somewhere in it, is true no matter what value in S x takes on. Often the â€œâˆˆ Sâ€ is left
out. For example, in a discussion of lists, you might be asked to prove âˆ€l.length l > 0 â‡’
âˆƒx. member(x, l). Obviously, l is a list, even if it isnâ€™t explicitly stated as such.
There are several choices as to how to prove a formula beginning with âˆ€x. The standard
thing to do is to just prove P (x), not assuming anything about x. Thus, in doing the proof
129
you sort of just mentally strip off the âˆ€x. What you would write when doing this is â€œLet x be
any Sâ€. However, there are some subtletiesâ€”if youâ€™re already using an x for something else,
you canâ€™t use the same x, because then you would be assuming something about x, namely
that it equals the x youâ€™re already using. In this case, you need to use alpha-conversion1 to
change the formula you want to prove to âˆ€y âˆˆ S.P (y), where y is some variable youâ€™re not
already using, and then prove P (y). What you could write in this case is â€œSince x is already
in use, weâ€™ll prove the property of yâ€.
An alternative is induction, if S is a set that is defined with a structural definition. Many
objects youâ€™re likely to be proving properties of are defined with a structural definition.
This includes natural numbers, lists, trees, and terms of a computer language. Sometimes
you can use induction over the natural numbers to prove things about other objects, such
as graphs, by inducting over the number of nodes (or edges) in a graph.
You use induction when you see that during the course of the proof you would need to use
the property P for the subparts of x in order to prove it for x. This usually ends up being
the case if P involves functions defined recursively (i.e., the return value for the function
depends on the function value on the subparts of the argument).
A special case of induction is case analysis. Itâ€™s basically induction where you donâ€™t use the
inductive hypothesis: you just prove the property for each possible form that x could have.
Case analysis can be used to prove the theorem about lists above.
A final possibility (which you can use for all formulas, not just for universally quantified
ones) is to assume the contrary, and then derive a contradiction.
âˆƒx âˆˆ S.P (x) This says â€œThere exists an x in S such that P holds of x.â€ Such a formula is
called an existentially quantified formula. The main way to prove this is to figure out what
x has to be (that is, to find a concrete representation of it), and then prove that P holds of
that value. Sometimes you canâ€™t give a completely specified value, since the value you pick
for x has to depend on the values of other things you have floating around. For example,
say you want to prove
âˆ€x, y âˆˆ â„œ.x < y âˆ§ sin x < 0 âˆ§ sin y > 0â‡’ âˆƒz.x < z âˆ§ z < y âˆ§ sin z = 0
where â„œ is the set of real numbers. By the time you get to dealing with the âˆƒz.x < z âˆ§ z <
y âˆ§ sin z = 0, you will have already assumed that x and y were any real numbers. Thus the
value you choose for z has to depend on whatever x and y are.
An alternative way to prove âˆƒx âˆˆ S.P (x) is, of course, to assume that no such x exists, and
derive a contradiction.
To summarize what Iâ€™ve gone over so far: to prove a universally quantified formula, you must
prove it for a generic variable, one that you havenâ€™t used before. To prove an existentially
quantified formula, you get to choose a value that you want to prove the property of.
P â‡’ Q This says â€œIf P is true, then Q is trueâ€. Such a formula is called an implication,
and it is often pronounced â€œP implies Qâ€. The part before the â‡’ sign (here P ) is called
the antecedent, and the part after the â‡’ sign (here Q) is called the consequent. P â‡’ Q is
equivalent to Â¬P âˆ¨Q, and so if P is false, or if Q is true, then P â‡’ Q is true.
The standard way to prove this is to assume P , then use it to help you prove Q. Note that
I said that you will be using P . Thus you will need to follow the rules in Section A.2.4 to
deal with the logical connectives in P .
Other ways to prove P â‡’ Q involve the fact that it is equivalent to Â¬P âˆ¨Q. Thus, you can
prove Â¬P without bothering with Q, or you can just prove Q without bothering with P .
1Alpha-equivalence says that the name of a bound variable doesnâ€™t matter, so you can change it at will
(this is called alpha-conversion). Youâ€™ll get to know the exact meaning of this soon enough so I wonâ€™t explain
this here.
130
To reason by contradiction you assume that P is true and that Q is not true, and derive a
contradiction.
Another alternative is to prove the contrapositive: Â¬Qâ‡’ Â¬P , which is equivalent to it.
P â‡” Q This says â€œP is true if and only if Q is trueâ€. The phrase â€œif and only ifâ€ is usually
abbreviated â€œiffâ€. Basically, this means that P and Q are either both true, or both false.
Iff is usually used in two main ways: one is where the equivalence is due to one formula
being a definition of another. For example, A âŠ† B â‡” (âˆ€x. x âˆˆ Aâ‡’ x âˆˆ B) is the standard
definition of subset. For these iff statements, you donâ€™t have to prove them. The other use
of iff is to state the equivalence of two different things. For example, you could define an
SML function fact:
fun fact 0 = 1
| fact n = n * fact (n - 1)
Since in SML whole numbers are integers (both positive and negative) you may be asked
to prove: fact x terminates â‡” x â‰¥ 0. The standard way to do this is us the equivalence
P â‡” Q is equivalent to P â‡’ Q âˆ§ Q â‡’ P . And so youâ€™d prove that (fact x terminates â‡’
x â‰¥ 0) âˆ§ (x â‰¥ 0â‡’ fact x terminates).
Â¬P This says â€œP is not trueâ€. It is equivalent to P â‡’ false, thus this is one of the ways
you prove it: you assume that P is true, and derive a contradiction (that is, you prove
false). Hereâ€™s an example of this, which youâ€™ll run into later this year: the undecidability
of the halting problem can be rephrased as Â¬âˆƒx âˆˆ RM. x solves the halting problem, where
RM is the set of register machines. The proof of this in your Computation Theory notes
follows exactly the pattern I describedâ€”it assumes there is such a machine and derives a
contradiction.
The other major way to prove Â¬P is to figure out what the negation of P is, using equiva-
lences like De Morganâ€™s Law, and then prove that. For example, to prove Â¬âˆ€x âˆˆ N . âˆƒy âˆˆ
N . x = y2, where N is the set of natural numbers, you could push in the negation to get:
âˆƒx âˆˆ N . âˆ€y âˆˆ N . x 6= y2, and then you could prove that.
P âˆ§Q This says â€œP is true and Q is trueâ€. Such a formula is called a conjunction. To
prove this, you have to prove P , and you have to prove Q.
P âˆ¨Q This says â€œP is true or Q is trueâ€. This is inclusive or: if P and Q are both true,
then P âˆ¨Q is still true. Such a formula is called a disjunction. To prove this, you can prove
P or you can prove Q. You have to choose which one to prove. For example, if you need to
prove (5 mod 2 = 0) âˆ¨ (5 mod 2 = 1), then youâ€™ll choose the second one and prove that.
However, as with existentials, the choice of which one to prove will often depend on the
values of other things, like universally quantified variables. For example, when you are
studying the theory of programming languages (you will get a bit of this in Semantics), you
might be asked to prove
âˆ€P âˆˆ ML. P is properly typedâ‡’
(the evaluation of P runs forever) âˆ¨ (P evaluates to a value)
where ML is the set of all ML programs. You donâ€™t know in advance which of these will be
the case, since some programs do run forever, and some do evaluate to a value. Generally,
the best way to prove the disjunction in this case (when you donâ€™t know in advance which
will hold) is to use the equivalence with implication. For example, you can use the fact
that P âˆ¨ Q is equivalent to Â¬P â‡’ Q, then assume Â¬P , then use this to prove Q. For
example, your best bet to proving this programming languages theorem is to assume that
the evaluation of P doesnâ€™t run forever, and use this to prove that P evaluates to a value.

A.2.4 How to Use a Formula
You often end up using a formula to prove other formulas. You can use a formula if someone
has already proved that itâ€™s true, or you are assuming it because it was in an implication,
namely, the A in Aâ‡’ B. For each logical connective, Iâ€™ll tell you how to use it.
âˆ€x âˆˆ S.P (x) This formula says that something is true of all elements of S. Thus, when
you use it, you can pick any value at all to use instead of x (call it v), and then you can use
P (v).
âˆƒx âˆˆ S.P (x) This formula says that there is some x that satisfies P . However, you do not
know what it is, so you can not assume anything about it. The usual approach it to just
say that the thing that is being said to exist is just x, and use the fact that P holds of x to
prove something else. However, if youâ€™re already using an x for something else, you have to
pick another variable to represent the thing that exists.
To summarize this: to use a universally quantified formula, you can choose any value, and
use that the formula holds for that variable. To use an existentially quantified formula, you
must not assume anything about the value that is said to exists, so you just use a variable
(one that you havenâ€™t used before) to represent it. Note that this is more or less opposite
of what you do when you prove a universally or existentially quantified formula.
Â¬P Usually, the main use of this formula is to prove the negation of something else.
An example is the use of reduction to prove the unsolvability of various problems in the
Computation Theory (youâ€™ll learn all about this in Lent term). You want to prove Â¬Q,
where Q states that a certain problem (Problem 1) is decidable (in other words, you want
to prove that Problem 1 is not decidable). You know Â¬P , where P states that another
problem (Problem 2) is decidable (i.e. Â¬P says that Problem 2 is not decidable). What you
do basically is this. You first prove Qâ‡’ P , which says that if Problem 1 is decidable, then
so is Problem 2. Since Qâ‡’ P â‰ƒ Â¬P â‡’ Â¬Q, you have now proved Â¬P â‡’ Â¬Q. You already
know Â¬P , so you use modus ponens2 to get that Â¬Q.
P â‡’ Q The main way to use this is that you prove P , and then you use modus ponens to
get Q, which you can then use.
P â‡” Q The main use of this is to replace an occurrence of P in a formula with Q, and
vise versa.
P âˆ§Q Here you can use both P and Q. Note, youâ€™re not required to use both of them, but
they are both true and are waiting to be used by you if you need them.
P âˆ¨Q Here, you know that one of P or Q is true, but you do not know which one. To use
this to prove something else, you have to do a split: first you prove the thing using P , then
you prove it using Q.
Note that in each of the above, there is again a difference in the way you use a formula,
verses the way you prove it. They are in a way almost opposites. For example, in proving
P âˆ§ Q, you have to prove both P and Q, but when you are using the formula, you donâ€™t
have to use both of them.
A.3 An Example
There are several exercises in the Semantics notes that ask you to prove something. Here,
weâ€™ll go back to Regular Languages and Finite Automata. (If theyâ€™ve faded, itâ€™s time
2Modus ponens says that if A â‡’ B and A are both true, then B is true.
132
to remind yourself of them.) The Pumping Lemma for regular sets (PL for short) is an
astonishingly good example of the use of quantifiers. Weâ€™ll go over the proof and use of the
PL, paying special attention to the logic of whatâ€™s happening.
A.3.1 Proving the PL
My favorite book on regular languages, finite automata, and their friends is the Hopcroft
and Ullman book Introduction to Automata Theory, Languages, and Computation. You
should locate this book in your college library, and if it isnâ€™t there, insist that your DoS
order it for you.
In the Automata Theory book, the Pumping Lemma is stated as: â€œLet L be a regular set.
Then there is a constant n such that if z is any word in L, and |z| â‰¥ n, we may write z = uvw
in such a way that |uv| â‰¤ n, |v| â‰¥ 1, and for all i â‰¥ 0, uviw is in L.â€ The Pumping Lemma
is, in my experience, one of the most difficult things about learning automata theory. It
is difficult because people donâ€™t know what to do with all those logical connectives. Letâ€™s
write it as a logical formula.
âˆ€L âˆˆ RegularLanguages.
âˆƒn. âˆ€z âˆˆ L. |z| â‰¥ nâ‡’
âˆƒu v w. z = uvw âˆ§ |uv| â‰¤ n âˆ§ |v| â‰¥ 1 âˆ§
âˆ€i â‰¥ 0. uviw âˆˆ L
Complicated, eh? Well, letâ€™s prove it, using the facts that Hopcroft and Ullman have
established in the chapters previous to the one wih the PL. Iâ€™ll give the proof and put in
square brackets comments about what Iâ€™m doing.
Let L be any regular language. [Here Iâ€™m dealing with the âˆ€L âˆˆ RegularLanguages by
stating that Iâ€™m not assuming anything about L.] Let M be a minimal-state deterministic
finite state machine accepting L. [Here Iâ€™m using a fact that Hopcroft and Ullman have
already proved about the equivalence of regular languages and finite automata.] Let n be
the number of states in this finite state machine. [Iâ€™m dealing with the âˆƒn by giving a very
specific value of what it will be, based on the arbitrary L.] Let z be any word in L. [Thus
I deal with âˆ€z âˆˆ L.] Assume that |z| â‰¥ n. [Thus Iâ€™m taking care of the â‡’ by assuming the
antecedent.]
Say z is written a1a2 . . . am, where m â‰¥ n. Consider the states that M is in during the
processing of the first n symbols of z, a1a2 . . . an. There are n + 1 of these states. Since
there are only n states in M , there must be a duplicate. Say that after symbols aj and ak
we are in the same state, state s (i.e. thereâ€™s a loop from this state that the machine goes
through as it accepts z), and say that j < k. Now, let u = a1a2 . . . aj . This represents the
part of the string that gets you to state s the first time. Let v = aj+1 . . . ak. This represents
the loop that takes you from s and back to it again. Let w = ak+1 . . . am, the rest of word
z. [We have chosen definite values for u, v, and w.] Then clearly z = uvw, since u, v, and
w are just different sections of z. |uv| â‰¤ n since u and v occur within the first n symbols
of z. |v| â‰¥ 1 since j < k. [Note that weâ€™re dealing with the formulas connected with âˆ§ by
proving each of them.]
Now, let i be a natural number (i.e. â‰¥ 0). [This deals with âˆ€i â‰¥ 0.] Then uviw âˆˆ L. [Finally
our conclusion, but we have to explain why this is true.] This is because we can repeat the
loop from s to s (represented by v) as many times as we like, and the resulting word will
still be accepted by M .
A.3.2 Using the PL
Now we use the PL to prove that a language is not regular. This is a rewording of Example
3.1 from Hopcroft and Ullman. Iâ€™ll show that L = {0i2 |i is an integer, i â‰¥ 1} is not regular.
Note that L consists of all strings of 0â€™s whose length is a perfect square. I will use the PL.
133
I want to prove that L is not regular. Iâ€™ll assume the negation (i.e., that L is regular) and
derive a contradiction. So here we go. Remember that what Iâ€™m emphasizing here is not
the finite automata stuff itself, but how to use a complicated theorem to prove something
else.
Assume L is regular. We will use the PL to get a contradiction. Since L is regular, the PL
applies to it. [We note that weâ€™re using the âˆ€ part of the PL for this particular L.] Let n
be as described in the PL. [This takes care of using the âˆƒn. Note that we are not assuming
anything about its actual value, just that itâ€™s a natural number.] Let z = 0n
2
. [Since the PL
says that something is true of all zs, we can choose the one we want to use it for.] So by the
PL there exist u, v, and w such that z = uvw, |uv| â‰¤ n, |v| â‰¥ 1. [Note that we donâ€™t assume
anything about what the u, v, and w actually are; the only thing we know about them is
what the PL tells us about them. This is where people trying to use the PL usually screw
up.] The PL then says that for any i, then uviw âˆˆ L. Well, then uv2w âˆˆ L. [This is using
the âˆ€i â‰¥ 0 bit.] However, n2 < |uv2w| â‰¤ n2 + n, since 1 â‰¤ |v| â‰¤ n. But n2 + n < (n + 1)2.
Thus |uv2w| lies properly between n2 and (n + 1)2 and is thus not a perfect square. Thus
uv2w is not in L. This is a contradiction. Thus our assumption (that L was regular) was
incorrect. Thus L is not a regular language.
A.4 Sequent Calculus Rules
In this section, I will show how the intuitive approach to things that Iâ€™ve described above
is reflected in the sequent calculus rules. A sequent is Î“ âŠ¢ âˆ†, where Î“ and âˆ† are sets of
formulas.3 Technically, this means that
A1 âˆ§A2 âˆ§ . . . An â‡’ B1 âˆ¨B2 âˆ¨ . . . Bm (1)
where A1, A2, . . . An are the formulas in Î“ , and B1, B2, . . . Bn are the formulas in âˆ†. Less
formally, this means â€œusing the formulas in Î“ we can prove that one of the formula in âˆ† is
true.â€ This is just the intuition I described above about using vs proving formulas, except
that I only talked about proving that one formula is true, rather than proving that one of
several formulas is true. In order to handle the âˆ¨ connective, there can be any number of
formulas on the right hand side of the âŠ¢.
For each logic connective,4 Iâ€™ll give the rules for it, and explain how it relates to the intuitive
way of using or proving formulas. For each connective there are at least two rules for it: one
for the left side of the âŠ¢, and one for the right side. This corresponds to having different
ways to treat a formula depending on whether youâ€™re using it (for formulas on the left hand
side of the âŠ¢) or proving it (for formulas on the right side of the âŠ¢).
Itâ€™s easiest to understand these rules from the bottom up. The conclusion of the rule (the
sequent below the horizontal line) is what we want to prove. The hypotheses of the rule
(the sequents above the horizontal line) are how we go about proving it. Weâ€™ll have to use
more rules, adding to the top, to build up the proof of the hypothesis, but this at least tells
us how to get going.
You can stop when the formula you have on the top is a basic sequent. This is Î“ âŠ¢ âˆ† where
thereâ€™s at least one formula (say P ) thatâ€™s in both Î“ and âˆ†. You can see why this is the
basic true formula: it says that if P and the other formulas in Î“ are true, then P or one of
the other formula in âˆ† is true.
In building proofs from these rules, there are several ways that you end up with formulas
to the left of the âŠ¢, where you can use them rather than proving them. One is that youâ€™ve
3In your Logic and Proof notes, the symbol that divides Î“ from âˆ† is â‡’. However, that conflicts with the
use of â‡’ as implication. Thus I will use âŠ¢. You will see something similar in Semantics, where it separates

already proved it before. This is shown with the cut rule:

The âˆ†, P in the first sequent in the hypotheses means that to the right of the âŠ¢ we have

true, then P or one of the formulas in âˆ† is true. Similarly P,Î“ to the left of the âŠ¢ in the
second sequent means the set consisting of the formula P plus all the formulas in Î“.
We read this rule from the bottom up to make sense of it. Say we want to prove one of the
formulas in âˆ† from the formulas in Î“, and we want to make use of a formula P that weâ€™ve
already proved. The fact that weâ€™ve proved P is shown by the left hypothesis (of course,
unless the left hypothesis is itself a basic sequent, then in a completed proof there will be
more lines on top of the left hypothesis, showing the actual proof of the sequent). The fact
that we are allowed to use P in the proof of âˆ† is shown in the right hand hypothesis. We
continue to build the proof up from there, using P .
Some other ways of getting formulas to the left of the âŠ¢ are shown in the rules (Â¬r) and
(â‡’ r) below.
âˆ€x âˆˆ S.P (x) The two rules for universally quantified formulas are:
P (v),Î“ âŠ¢ âˆ†
âˆ€x.P (x),Î“ âŠ¢ âˆ† (âˆ€l)
Î“ âŠ¢ âˆ†, P (x)
Î“ âŠ¢ âˆ†,âˆ€x.P (x) (âˆ€r)
In the (âˆ€r) rule, x must not be free in the conclusion.
Now, whatâ€™s going on here? In the (âˆ€l) rule, the âˆ€x.P (x) is on the left side of the âŠ¢. Thus, we
are using it (along with some other formula, those in Î“) to prove something (âˆ†). According
to the intuition above, in order to use âˆ€x.P (x), you can use it with any value, where v is
used to represent that value. In the hypothesis, you see the formula P (v) to the left of the
âŠ¢. This is just P with v substituted for x. The use of this corresponds exactly to using the
fact that P is true of any value whatsoever, since we are using it with v, which is any value
of our choice.
In the (âˆ€r) rule, the âˆ€x.P (x) is on the right side of the âŠ¢. Thus, we are proving it. Thus,
we need to prove it for a generic x. This is why the âˆ€x is gone in the hypothesis. The x
is still sitting somewhere in the P , but weâ€™re just using it as a plain variable, not assuming
anything about it. And this explains the side condition too: â€œIn the (âˆ€r) rule, x must not
be free in the conclusion.â€ If x is not free in the conclusion, this means that x is not free in
the formulas in Î“ or âˆ†. That means the only place the x occurs free in the hypothesis is in
P itself. This corresponds exactly with the requirement that weâ€™re proving that P is true
of a generic x: if x were free in Î“ or âˆ†, we would be assuming something about x, namely
that value of x is the same as the x used in those formulas.
Note that induction is not mentioned in the rules. This is because the sequent calculus used
here just deals with pure logic. In more complicated presentations of logic, it is explained
how to define new types via structural induction, and from there you get mechanisms to
allow you to do induction.
âˆƒx âˆˆ S.P (x) The two rules for existentially quantified formulas are:
P (x),Î“ âŠ¢ âˆ†

Î“ âŠ¢ âˆ†, P (v)

In the (âˆƒl) rule, x must not be free in the conclusion.
In (âˆƒl), we are using âˆƒx.P (x). Thus we cannot assume anything about the value that the
formula says exists, so we just use it as x in the hypothesis. The side condition about x not

being free in the conclusions comes from the requirement not to assume anything about x

In (âˆƒr), we are proving âˆƒx.P (x). Thus we must pick a particular value (call it v) and prove

Letâ€™s start with the right rule first. I said that the way to prove Â¬P is to assume P and

you have derived a contradiction. So if âˆ† is the empty set, the hypothesis of the rule says
that, assuming P , you have obtained a contradiction. Thus, if you are absolutely certain

understand the rule if âˆ† is not empty is to write out the meaning of the sequents in terms

The easiest way to understand (Â¬l) is again by using equivalences.
P â‡’ Q The two rules for implication are:

The rule (â‡’ l) easily understood using the intuitive explanation of how to use P â‡’ Q given

The right rule (â‡’ r) is also easily understood. In order to prove P â‡’ Q, we assume P ,
then use this to prove Q. This is exactly what the hypothesis says.

Both of these rules are easily explained by the intuition above. The left rule (âˆ§l) says that

These are also easily understood by the intuitive explanations above. The left rule says that
